[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Sk%C5%82adkowski_premierem.jpg/260px-Sk%C5%82adkowski_premierem.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Składkowski_premierem.jpg)

A year after Piłsudski's death, his former personal assistant General [Felicjan Sławoj Składkowski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felicjan_Sławoj_Składkowski) became the [Second Polish Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic)'s last prime minister

After Piłsudski's death in 1935, Poland was governed until (and initially during) the [German invasion of 1939](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland) by old allies and subordinates known as "[Piłsudski's colonels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piłsudski's_colonels)". They had neither the vision nor the resources to cope with the perilous situation facing Poland in the late 1930s. The colonels had gradually assumed greater powers during Piłsudski's life by manipulating the ailing marshal behind the scenes.[[129\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-129) Eventually they achieved an overt politicization of the army that did nothing to help prepare the country for war.[[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_291–321-84)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Beck_przem%C3%B3wienie.jpg/170px-Beck_przem%C3%B3wienie.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Beck_przemówienie.jpg)

Foreign Minister [Józef Beck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Józef_Beck) rejected the proposed risky alliances with [Nazi Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazi_Germany) and with the [Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union)[[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_291–321-84)

Foreign policy was the responsibility of [Józef Beck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Józef_Beck), under whom Polish diplomacy attempted balanced approaches toward Germany and the Soviet Union, unfortunately without success, on the basis of a flawed understanding of the European geopolitics of his day. Beck had numerous foreign policy schemes and harbored illusions of Poland's status as a great power. He alienated most of Poland's neighbors, but is not blamed by historians for the ultimate failure of relations with Germany. The principal events of his tenure were concentrated in its last two years. In the case of the [1938 Polish ultimatum to Lithuania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1938_Polish_ultimatum_to_Lithuania), the Polish action nearly resulted in a German takeover of southwest [Lithuania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithuania), the [Klaipėda Region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Klaipėda_Region) (Memel Territory), which had a largely German population.[[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-130) Also in 1938, the Polish government opportunistically undertook a hostile action against the [Czechoslovak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Czechoslovakia) state as weakened by the [Munich Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Munich_Agreement) and [annexed a small piece of territory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish–Czechoslovak_border_conflicts) on its borders.[[131\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-131) In this case, Beck's understanding of the consequences of the Polish military move turned out to be completely mistaken,[[132\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-132)[[133\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Jak_Polacy_i_Rosjanie_młócą_historię-133) because in the end the [German occupation of Czechoslovakia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_occupation_of_Czechoslovakia) markedly weakened Poland's own position.[[134\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-134) Furthermore, Beck erroneously believed that Nazi-Soviet ideological contradictions would preclude their cooperation.[[135\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zgórniak_455–465-135)

At home, increasingly alienated and suppressed minorities threatened unrest and violence. Extreme nationalist circles such as the [National Radical Camp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Radical_Camp_(1934)) grew more outspoken. One of the groups, the [Camp of National Unity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Camp_of_National_Unity), combined many nationalists with Sanation supporters and was connected to the new strongman, Marshal [Edward Rydz-Śmigły](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Rydz-Śmigły), whose faction of the Sanation ruling movement was increasingly nationalistic.[[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_291–321-84)[[136\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-136)[[137\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Heart_127-129-137)[[138\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_361–365-138)

In the late 1930s, the exile bloc [Front Morges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Front_Morges) united several major Polish anti-Sanation figures, including [Ignacy Paderewski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ignacy_Jan_Paderewski), [Władysław Sikorski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Władysław_Sikorski), [Wincenty Witos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wincenty_Witos), [Wojciech Korfanty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wojciech_Korfanty) and [Józef Haller](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Józef_Haller). It gained little influence inside Poland, but its spirit soon reappeared during [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II), within the [Polish government-in-exile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_government-in-exile).[[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_291–321-84)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Ulica_Marsza%C5%82kowska_w_Warszawie_przed_1939a.jpg/185px-Ulica_Marsza%C5%82kowska_w_Warszawie_przed_1939a.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ulica_Marszałkowska_w_Warszawie_przed_1939a.jpg)

[Warsaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw) was one of Europe's chief cities before the [Second World War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_World_War), pictured in 1939

In October 1938, [Joachim von Ribbentrop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joachim_von_Ribbentrop) first proposed German-Polish territorial adjustments and Poland's participation in the [Anti-Comintern Pact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-Comintern_Pact) against the Soviet Union.[[139\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-139) The status of the [Free City of Danzig](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_City_of_Danzig) was one of the key bones of contention. Approached by Ribbentrop again in March 1939, the Polish government expressed willingness to address issues causing German concern, but effectively rejected Germany's stated demands and thus refused to allow Poland to be turned by [Adolf Hitler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adolf_Hitler) into a German [puppet state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Puppet_state).[[140\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zgórniak_422–425-140) Hitler, incensed by the British and French declarations of support for Poland,[[140\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zgórniak_422–425-140) abrogated the [German–Polish declaration of non-aggression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German–Polish_declaration_of_non-aggression) in late April 1939.[[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_291–321-84)[[135\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zgórniak_455–465-135)[[141\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-141)

To protect itself from an increasingly aggressive [Nazi Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazi_Germany), already responsible for the annexations of [Austria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Austria) (in the [Anschluss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anschluss) of 1938), Czechoslovakia (in 1939) and a part of Lithuania after the [1939 German ultimatum to Lithuania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1939_German_ultimatum_to_Lithuania), Poland entered into a military alliance with Britain and France (the 1939 [Anglo-Polish military alliance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Polish_military_alliance) and the [Franco-Polish alliance (1921)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franco-Polish_alliance_(1921)), as updated in 1939).[[142\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-142) However, the two Western powers were defense-oriented and not in a strong position, either geographically or in terms of resources, to assist Poland. Attempts were therefore made by them to induce Soviet-Polish cooperation, which they viewed as the only militarily viable arrangement.[[143\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_319-320-143)[[144\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-144)

Diplomatic manoeuvers continued in the spring and summer of 1939, but in their final attempts, the Franco-British talks with the Soviets in Moscow on forming an anti-Nazi defensive military alliance failed. Warsaw's refusal to allow the [Red Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_Army) to operate on Polish territory doomed the Western efforts.[[145\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-145) The final contentious Allied-Soviet exchanges took place on 21 and 23 August 1939.[[135\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zgórniak_455–465-135)[[146\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stalin_million_troops-146)[[147\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Heart_155-156-147)[[b\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_bnone) The regime of [Joseph Stalin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Stalin) was the target of an intense German counter-initiative and was concurrently involved in increasingly effective negotiations with Hitler's agents. On 23 August, an outcome contrary to the exertions of the Allies became a reality: in Moscow, Germany and the Soviet Union hurriedly signed the [Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molotov–Ribbentrop_Pact), which secretly provided for the dismemberment of Poland into Nazi- and Soviet-controlled zones.[[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_291–321-84)[[135\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zgórniak_455–465-135)[[137\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Heart_127-129-137)

## World War II

### Invasions and resistance

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Schleswig_Holstein_firing_Westerplatte_September_1939.jpg/220px-Schleswig_Holstein_firing_Westerplatte_September_1939.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Schleswig_Holstein_firing_Westerplatte_September_1939.jpg)

German [battleship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battleship) [*Schleswig-Holstein*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SMS_Schleswig-Holstein) [shells Westerplatte](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Westerplatte), 1 September 1939

On 1 September 1939, Hitler ordered an [invasion of Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland), the opening event of [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II). Poland had signed an [Anglo-Polish military alliance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Polish_military_alliance) as recently as the 25th of August, and had long been in [alliance with France](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franco-Polish_alliance_(1921)). The two Western powers soon declared war on Germany, but they remained largely inactive (the period early in the conflict became known as the [Phoney War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phoney_War)) and extended no aid to the attacked country. The technically and numerically superior *[Wehrmacht](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wehrmacht)* formations rapidly advanced eastwards and engaged massively in the murder of Polish civilians over the entire occupied territory.[[148\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Wrzesień_'39-148) On 17 September, a [Soviet invasion of Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Poland) began. The Soviet Union quickly occupied most of the areas of eastern Poland that were inhabited by a significant [Ukrainian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukrainians) and [Belarusian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belarusians) minority.[[h\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_hnone) The two invading powers divided up the country as they had agreed in the secret provisions of the [Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molotov–Ribbentrop_Pact). Poland's top government officials and military high command fled the war zone and arrived at the [Romanian Bridgehead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanian_Bridgehead) in mid-September. After the Soviet entry they sought refuge in [Romania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romania).[[149\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-149)[[150\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Biskupski_214-215-150)[[151\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Kochanski_59-93-151)

Among the military operations in which Poles held out the longest (until late September or early October) were the [Siege of Warsaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siege_of_Warsaw_(1939)), the [Battle of Hel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Hel) and the resistance of the [Independent Operational Group Polesie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Independent_Operational_Group_Polesie). Warsaw fell on 27 September after a heavy German bombardment that killed tens of thousands civilians and soldiers.[[151\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Kochanski_59-93-151) Poland was ultimately partitioned between Germany and the Soviet Union according to the terms of the [German–Soviet Frontier Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German–Soviet_Frontier_Treaty) signed by the two powers in Moscow on 29 September.[[152\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-152)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Poland_in_1939.jpg/220px-Poland_in_1939.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Poland_in_1939.jpg)

Map of Poland following the [German and Soviet invasions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland) (1939)

[Gerhard Weinberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gerhard_Weinberg) has argued that the most significant [Polish contribution to World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_contribution_to_World_War_II) was sharing its code-breaking results.[[153\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-153) This allowed the British to perform the [cryptanalysis of the Enigma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cryptanalysis_of_the_Enigma) and decipher the main German military code, which gave the [Allies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II) a major advantage in the conflict.[[154\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Winberg_2005_50-154) As regards actual military campaigns, some Polish historians have argued that simply resisting the initial invasion of Poland was the country's greatest contribution to the victory over Nazi Germany, despite its defeat. The Polish Army of nearly one million men significantly delayed the start of the [Battle of France](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_France), planned by the Germans for 1939. When the Nazi offensive in the West did happen, the delay caused it to be less effective, a possibly crucial factor in the victory of the [Battle of Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Britain).[[155\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_693–694-155)

After Germany invaded the Soviet Union as part of its [Operation Barbarossa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa) in June 1941, the whole of pre-war Poland was overrun and occupied by German troops.[[156\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-156)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Dywizjon_303_in_color.jpg/220px-Dywizjon_303_in_color.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Dywizjon_303_in_color.jpg)

Pilots of [No. 303 Polish Fighter Squadron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/No._303_Polish_Fighter_Squadron) won fame in the [Battle of Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Britain)

[German-occupied Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occupation_of_Poland_(1939–1945)) was divided from 1939 into two regions: [Polish areas annexed by Nazi Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_areas_annexed_by_Nazi_Germany) directly into the [German *Reich*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_Reich) and areas ruled under a so-called [General Government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Government) of occupation.[[157\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_326-346-157) The Poles formed an [underground resistance movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_resistance_movement_in_World_War_II) and a [Polish government-in-exile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_government-in-exile) that operated first in [Paris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paris), then, from July 1940, in [London](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/London).[[158\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Czubiński_226-158) Polish-Soviet diplomatic relations, broken since September 1939, were resumed in July 1941 under the [Sikorski–Mayski agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sikorski–Mayski_agreement), which facilitated the formation of a Polish army (the [Anders' Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anders'_Army)) in the Soviet Union.[[159\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-159)[[160\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-160) In November 1941, Prime Minister Sikorski flew to the Soviet Union to negotiate with Stalin on its role on the [Soviet-German front](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)), but the British wanted the Polish soldiers in the [Middle East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_East). Stalin agreed, and the army was evacuated there.[[161\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-161)[[162\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-162)[[w\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_wnone)

The organizations forming the [Polish Underground State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Underground_State) that functioned in Poland throughout the war were loyal to and formally under the Polish government-in-exile, acting through its [Government Delegation for Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_Delegation_for_Poland).[[163\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_344-346-163) During World War II, hundreds of thousands of Poles joined the underground Polish [Home Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Home_Army) (*Armia Krajowa*),[[164\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-164) a part of the [Polish Armed Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Armed_Forces_in_the_West) of the government-in-exile.[[158\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Czubiński_226-158) About 200,000 Poles fought on the [Western Front](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_II)) in the [Polish Armed Forces in the West](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Armed_Forces_in_the_West) loyal to the government-in-exile, and about 300,000 in the [Polish Armed Forces in the East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Armed_Forces_in_the_East#Berling_Army:_1943–1945) under the Soviet command on the [Eastern Front](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)).[[155\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_693–694-155) The pro-Soviet resistance movement in Poland, led by the [Polish Workers' Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Workers'_Party), was active from 1941. It was opposed by the gradually forming extreme nationalistic [National Armed Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Armed_Forces).[[158\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Czubiński_226-158)[[t\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_tnone)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Stroop_Report_-_Warsaw_Ghetto_Uprising_09.jpg/220px-Stroop_Report_-_Warsaw_Ghetto_Uprising_09.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Stroop_Report_-_Warsaw_Ghetto_Uprising_09.jpg)

[Warsaw Ghetto Uprising](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Ghetto_Uprising)

Beginning in late 1939, hundreds of thousands of Poles from the Soviet-occupied areas were deported and taken east. Of the upper-ranking military personnel and others deemed uncooperative or potentially harmful by the Soviets, about 22,000 were secretly executed by them at the [Katyn massacre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Katyn_massacre).[[165\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-165) In April 1943, the Soviet Union broke off deteriorating relations with the Polish government-in-exile after the German military announced the discovery of mass graves containing murdered Polish army officers. The Soviets claimed that the Poles committed a hostile act by requesting that the [Red Cross](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement) investigate these reports.[[166\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-166)

From 1941, the implementation of the [Nazi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazi_Party) [Final Solution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Final_Solution) began, and [the Holocaust in Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Holocaust_in_Poland) proceeded with force.[[167\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_337-343-167) Warsaw was the scene of the [Warsaw Ghetto Uprising](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Ghetto_Uprising) in April–May 1943, triggered by the liquidation of the [Warsaw Ghetto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Ghetto) by German [SS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schutzstaffel) units. The elimination of [Jewish ghettos in German-occupied Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_ghettos_in_German-occupied_Poland) took place in many cities. As the Jewish people were being removed to be exterminated, uprisings were waged against impossible odds by the [Jewish Combat Organization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_Combat_Organization) and other desperate Jewish insurgents.[[168\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-168)

### Soviet advance 1944–1945, Warsaw Uprising

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Sikorski_in_Gibraltar.jpg/220px-Sikorski_in_Gibraltar.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sikorski_in_Gibraltar.jpg)

Gen. [Władysław Sikorski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Władysław_Sikorski), prime minister of the [Polish government-in-exile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_government-in-exile) and commander-in-chief of [Polish armed forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_contribution_to_World_War_II), shortly before his death in 1943

At a time of increasing cooperation between the Western Allies and the Soviet Union in the wake of the [Nazi invasion of 1941](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa), the influence of the [Polish government-in-exile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_government-in-exile) was seriously diminished by the death of Prime Minister [Władysław Sikorski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Władysław_Sikorski), its most capable leader, in a [plane crash on 4 July 1943](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1943_Gibraltar_B-24_crash).[[169\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-169) Around that time, Polish-communist civilian and military organizations opposed to the government, led by [Wanda Wasilewska](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wanda_Wasilewska) and supported by Stalin, were formed in the Soviet Union.[[170\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Kochanski_371-377-170)

In July 1944, the Soviet [Red Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_Army) and Soviet-controlled [Polish People's Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_People's_Army) entered the territory of future postwar Poland. In protracted fighting in 1944 and 1945, the Soviets and their Polish allies defeated and expelled the German army from Poland at a cost of over 600,000 Soviet soldiers lost.[[171\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Buszko_394-395-171)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Warsaw_Uprising_Surrender-_5_of_October_1944.jpg/220px-Warsaw_Uprising_Surrender-_5_of_October_1944.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Warsaw_Uprising_Surrender-_5_of_October_1944.jpg)

Surrender of the [Warsaw Uprising](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Uprising)

The greatest single undertaking of the [Polish resistance movement in World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_resistance_movement_in_World_War_II) and a major political event was the [Warsaw Uprising](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Uprising) that began on 1 August 1944. The uprising, in which most of the city's population participated, was instigated by the underground [Home Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Home_Army) and approved by the Polish government-in-exile in an attempt to establish a non-communist Polish administration ahead of the arrival of the Red Army. The uprising was originally planned as a short-lived armed demonstration in expectation that the Soviet forces approaching Warsaw would assist in any battle to take the city.[[172\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-172) The Soviets had never agreed to an intervention, however, and they halted their advance at the [Vistula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vistula) River. The Germans used the opportunity to carry out a brutal suppression of the forces of the pro-Western Polish underground.[[173\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-173)[[174\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_4-5-174)[[m\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_mnone)

The bitterly fought uprising lasted for two months and resulted in the death or expulsion from the city of hundreds of thousands of civilians. After the defeated Poles surrendered on 2 October, the Germans carried out a [planned destruction of Warsaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planned_destruction_of_Warsaw) on Hitler's orders that obliterated the remaining infrastructure of the city. The [Polish First Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Polish_Army_(1944–1945)), fighting alongside the Soviet Red Army, entered a devastated Warsaw on 17 January 1945.[[174\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_4-5-174)[[175\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-175)[[n\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_nnone)

### Allied conferences, Polish governments

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Swierczewski_Spychalski_Zymierski.jpg/170px-Swierczewski_Spychalski_Zymierski.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Swierczewski_Spychalski_Zymierski.jpg)

[Polish generals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_People's_Army) on the [Eastern Front](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II))

From the time of the [Tehran Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tehran_Conference) in late 1943, there was broad agreement among the three Great Powers (the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States), the [United Kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom), and the [Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union)) that the locations of the borders between Germany and Poland and between Poland and the Soviet Union would be [fundamentally changed after the conclusion of World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Territorial_changes_of_Poland_immediately_after_World_War_II).[[176\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-176)[[177\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_1-177) Stalin's view that Poland should be moved far to the west was accepted by Polish communists, whose organizations included the [Polish Workers' Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Workers'_Party) and the [Union of Polish Patriots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Union_of_Polish_Patriots). The communist-led [State National Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_National_Council), a quasi-parliamentary body, was in existence in Warsaw from the beginning of 1944.[[178\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-178) In July 1944, a communist-controlled [Polish Committee of National Liberation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Committee_of_National_Liberation) was established in [Lublin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lublin), to nominally govern the areas liberated from German control. The move prompted protests from Prime Minister [Stanisław Mikołajczyk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanisław_Mikołajczyk) and his Polish government-in-exile.[[171\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Buszko_394-395-171)[[174\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_4-5-174)

By the time of the [Yalta Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yalta_Conference) in February 1945, the communists had already established a [Provisional Government of the Republic of Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_the_Republic_of_Poland). The Soviet position at the conference was strong because of their decisive contribution to the war effort and as a result of their occupation of immense amounts of land in central and eastern Europe. The Great Powers gave assurances that the communist provisional government would be converted into an entity that would include democratic forces from within the country and active abroad, but the London-based [government-in-exile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_government-in-exile) was not mentioned. A [Provisional Government of National Unity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_National_Unity) and subsequent democratic elections were the agreed stated goals.[[179\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-179)[[180\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_6-7-180) The disappointing results of these plans and the failure of the Western powers to ensure a strong participation of non-communists in the immediate post-war Polish government were seen by many Poles as a manifestation of [Western betrayal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_betrayal).

### War losses, extermination of Jews and Poles

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Samuel_Willenberg_Treblinka_2_sierpnia_2013_01.JPG/220px-Samuel_Willenberg_Treblinka_2_sierpnia_2013_01.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Samuel_Willenberg_Treblinka_2_sierpnia_2013_01.JPG)

[Samuel Willenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samuel_Willenberg) showing his drawings of the [Treblinka extermination camp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treblinka_extermination_camp)

A lack of accurate data makes it difficult to document numerically the extent of the human losses suffered by Polish citizens during World War II. Additionally, many assertions made in the past must be considered suspect due to flawed methodology and a desire to promote certain political agendas. The last available enumeration of ethnic Poles and the large ethnic minorities is the [Polish census of 1931](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_census_of_1931). Exact population figures for 1939 are therefore not known.[[181\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_694-695-181)[[182\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Polskosc_nosze_z_soba_w_plecaku-182)

According to the [United States Holocaust Memorial Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Holocaust_Memorial_Museum), at least 3 million Polish [Jews](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jews) and at least 1.9 million non-Jewish Polish civilians were killed.[[183\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Polish_victims-183) According to the historians Brzoza and Sowa, about 2 million ethnic [Poles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poles) were killed, but it is not known, even approximately, how many Polish citizens of other [ethnicities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethnic_group) perished, including [Ukrainians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukrainians), [Belarusians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belarusians), and [Germans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germans).[[184\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_695-696-184) Millions of Polish citizens were deported to Germany for forced labor or to German [extermination camps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extermination_camp) such as [Treblinka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treblinka_extermination_camp), [Auschwitz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auschwitz_concentration_camp) and [Sobibór](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sobibór_extermination_camp). Nazi Germany intended to exterminate the Jews completely, in actions that have come to be described collectively as [the Holocaust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Holocaust).[[185\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-185) The Poles were to be expelled from areas controlled by Nazi Germany through a process of [resettlement that started in 1939](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expulsion_of_Poles_by_Nazi_Germany). Such Nazi operations matured into a plan known as the *[Generalplan Ost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Generalplan_Ost)* that amounted to displacement, enslavement and partial extermination of the [Slavic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavs) people and was expected to be completed within 15 years.[[186\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-186)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Warsaw_Old_Town_1945.jpg/220px-Warsaw_Old_Town_1945.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Warsaw_Old_Town_1945.jpg)

[Warsaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw) destroyed, photo taken January 1945

The majority of Poles remained indifferent to the Jewish plight, and neither assisted nor persecuted Jews.[[187\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Winstone_2014-187)[[188\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Connelly_2005-188) Of those who have helped [rescue, shelter and protect Jews from the Nazi atrocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rescue_of_Jews_by_Poles_during_the_Holocaust), [Yad Vashem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yad_Vashem) and the [State of Israel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_of_Israel) have recognized 6,992 individuals as *[Righteous Among the Nations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Righteous_Among_the_Nations)*.[[189\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-YV_Stats-189)

In an attempt to incapacitate Polish society, the Nazis and the Soviets executed tens of thousands of members of the [intelligentsia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intelligentsia) and community leadership during events such as the [German AB-Aktion in Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_AB-Aktion_in_Poland), [Operation Tannenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Tannenberg) and the [Katyn massacre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Katyn_massacre).[[190\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-190)[[j\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_jnone) Over 95% of the Jewish losses and 90% of the ethnic Polish losses were caused directly by Nazi Germany,[[d\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_dnone) whereas 5% of the ethnic Polish losses were caused by the Soviets and 5% by Ukrainian nationalists.[[184\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_695-696-184) The large-scale [Jewish presence in Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Poland) that had endured for centuries was rather quickly put to an end by the policies of extermination implemented by the Nazis during the war. Waves of displacement and emigration that took place both during and after the war removed from Poland a majority of the Jews who survived. Further significant Jewish emigration followed events such as the [Polish October](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_October) political thaw of 1956 and the [1968 Polish political crisis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1968_Polish_political_crisis).[[191\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_157-163-191)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Birkenau_5.JPG/220px-Birkenau_5.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Birkenau_5.JPG)

The infamous gatehouse at [Auschwitz-Birkenau](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auschwitz_concentration_camp) concentration camp, where at least 1.1 million people were murdered by the Nazi regime

In 1940–1941, some 325,000 Polish citizens were deported by the Soviet regime.[[184\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_695-696-184) The number of Polish citizens who died at the hands of the Soviets is estimated at less than 100,000.[[184\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_695-696-184)

In 1943–1944, Ukrainian nationalists associated with the [Organization of Ukrainian Nationalists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organization_of_Ukrainian_Nationalists) (OUN) and the [Ukrainian Insurgent Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukrainian_Insurgent_Army) perpetrated the [Massacres of Poles in Volhynia and Eastern Galicia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Massacres_of_Poles_in_Volhynia_and_Eastern_Galicia).[[184\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_695-696-184) Estimates of the number of Polish civilian victims vary greatly, from tens to hundreds of thousands.[[192\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Motyka-192)

Approximately 90% of Poland's war casualties were the victims of prisons, death camps, raids, executions, the annihilation of ghettos, epidemics, starvation, excessive work and ill treatment. The war left one million children orphaned and 590,000 persons disabled. The country lost 38% of its national assets (whereas Britain lost only 0.8%, and France only 1.5%).[[193\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-193) Nearly half of pre-war Poland was expropriated by the Soviet Union, including the two great cultural centers of [Lwów](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lviv) and [Wilno](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vilnius).[[181\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_694-695-181)

The policies of Nazi Germany have been judged after the war by the International Military Tribunal at the [Nuremberg trials](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuremberg_trials) and Polish genocide trials to be aimed at extermination of Jews, Poles and Roma, and to have "all the characteristics of genocide in the biological meaning of this term".[[194\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-194)

### Changing boundaries and population transfers

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PKWN_Manifest.jpg/170px-PKWN_Manifest.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:PKWN_Manifest.jpg)

The [PKWN Manifesto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PKWN_Manifesto), officially issued on 22 July 1944 in Soviet-liberated Poland. It heralded the arrival of a communist government imposed by the [USSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/USSR).

By the terms of the 1945 [Potsdam Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potsdam_Agreement) signed by the three victorious Great Powers, the Soviet Union retained most of the territories captured as a result of the [Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molotov–Ribbentrop_Pact) of 1939, including western Ukraine and western Belarus, and gained others. [Lithuania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithuania) and the [Königsberg area of East Prussia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaliningrad_Oblast) were officially incorporated into the Soviet Union, in the case of the former without the recognition of the Western powers.

Poland was compensated with the bulk of [Silesia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silesia), including [Breslau (Wrocław)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wrocław) and [Grünberg (Zielona Góra)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zielona_Góra), the bulk of [Pomerania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pomerania), including [Stettin (Szczecin)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Szczecin), and the greater southern portion of the former [East Prussia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Prussia), along with [Danzig (Gdańsk)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gdańsk), pending a final peace conference with Germany which eventually never took place.[[195\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-195) Collectively referred to by the Polish authorities as the "[Recovered Territories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recovered_Territories)", they were included in the reconstituted Polish state. With Germany's defeat Poland was thus shifted west in relation to its prewar location, to the area between the [Oder–Neisse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oder–Neisse_line) and [Curzon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curzon_Line) lines, which resulted in a country more compact and with much broader access to the sea.[[c\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_cnone) The Poles lost 70% of their pre-war oil capacity to the Soviets, but gained from the Germans a highly developed industrial base and infrastructure that made a diversified industrial economy possible for the first time in Polish history.[[196\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-196)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Curzon_line_en.svg/220px-Curzon_line_en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Curzon_line_en.svg)

[Territorial changes of Poland immediately after World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Territorial_changes_of_Poland_immediately_after_World_War_II): the gray territories were transferred from Poland to the Soviet Union, whereas the pink territories were transferred from Germany to Poland. Poland's new eastern border was adjusted in the following years.

The [flight and expulsion of Germans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flight_and_expulsion_of_Germans_from_Poland_during_and_after_World_War_II) from what was eastern Germany prior to the war began before and during the Soviet conquest of those regions from the Nazis, and the process continued in the years immediately after the war.[[197\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-197) 8,030,000 Germans were evacuated, expelled, or migrated by 1950.[[198\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-198)

Early expulsions in Poland were undertaken by the Polish communist authorities even before the Potsdam Conference (the "wild expulsions" from June to mid July 1945, when the Polish military and militia expelled nearly all people from the districts immediately east of the [Oder–Neisse line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oder–Neisse_line)),[[199\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-199) to ensure the establishment of ethnically homogeneous Poland.[[200\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Gibney197-200)[[201\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-201) About 1% (100,000) of the German civilian population east of the Oder–Neisse line perished in the fighting prior to the [surrender in May 1945](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/End_of_World_War_II_in_Europe),[[202\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-202) and afterwards some 200,000 Germans in Poland were employed as forced labor prior to being expelled.[[203\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-203) Many Germans died in [labor camps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forced_labor_of_Germans_after_World_War_II) such as the [Zgoda labour camp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zgoda_labour_camp) and the [Potulice camp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Labour_Camp_in_Potulice). Of those Germans who remained within the new borders of Poland, many later chose to [emigrate to post-war Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flight_and_expulsion_of_Germans_(1944–1950)).

On the other hand, 1.5–2 million ethnic Poles moved or were expelled from the previously Polish [areas annexed by the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kresy). The vast majority were resettled in the [former German territories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recovered_Territories).[[204\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-204) At least one million Poles remained in what had become the Soviet Union, and at least half a million ended up in the West or elsewhere outside of Poland.[[184\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_695-696-184) However, contrary to the official declaration that the former German inhabitants of the [Recovered Territories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recovered_Territories) had to be removed quickly to house Poles displaced by the Soviet annexation, the [Recovered Territories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recovered_Territories) initially faced a severe population shortage.[[205\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-205)

Many exiled Poles could not return to the country for which they had fought because they belonged to political groups incompatible with the new communist regimes, or because they originated from areas of pre-war eastern Poland that were incorporated into the Soviet Union (see [Polish population transfers (1944–1946)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_population_transfers_(1944–1946))). Some were deterred from returning simply on the strength of warnings that anyone who had served in military units in the West would be endangered. Many Poles were pursued, arrested, tortured and imprisoned by the Soviet authorities for belonging to the [Home Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Home_Army) or other formations (see [Anti-communist resistance in Poland (1944–1946)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-communist_resistance_in_Poland_(1944–1946))),[[206\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_23-24-206) or were persecuted because they had fought on the Western front.[[207\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-207)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Bundesarchiv_Bild_175-S00-00326%2C_Fl%C3%BCchtlinge_aus_Ostpreu%C3%9Fen_auf_Pferdewagen.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_175-S00-00326%2C_Fl%C3%BCchtlinge_aus_Ostpreu%C3%9Fen_auf_Pferdewagen.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_175-S00-00326,_Flüchtlinge_aus_Ostpreußen_auf_Pferdewagen.jpg)

German refugees fleeing from [East Prussia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Prussia), 1945

Territories on both sides of the new Polish-Ukrainian border were also "[ethnically cleansed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing)". Of the Ukrainians and [Lemkos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lemkos) living in Poland within the new borders (about 700,000), close to 95% were [forcibly moved](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Poland_and_Soviet_Ukraine) to the [Soviet Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukrainian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic), or (in 1947) to the new territories in northern and western Poland under [Operation Vistula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Vistula). In [Volhynia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volhynia), 98% of the Polish pre-war population was either killed or expelled; in Eastern [Galicia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galicia_(Eastern_Europe)), the Polish population was reduced by 92%.[[208\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-208) According to [Timothy D. Snyder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timothy_D._Snyder), about 70,000 Poles and about 20,000 Ukrainians were killed in the ethnic violence that occurred in the 1940s, both during and after the war.[[209\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Snyder_204-205-209)

According to an estimate by historian [Jan Grabowski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jan_Grabowski_(historian)), about 50,000 of the 250,000 Polish Jews who escaped the Nazis during the liquidation of ghettos survived without leaving Poland (the remainder perished).[[210\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-210) More were repatriated from the Soviet Union and elsewhere, and the February 1946 population census showed about 300,000 Jews within Poland's new borders.[[211\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Buszko_410-211)[[e\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_enone) Of the surviving Jews, many chose to emigrate or felt compelled to because of the [anti-Jewish violence in Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-Jewish_violence_in_Poland,_1944–1946).[[212\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-212)

Because of changing borders and the mass movements of people of various nationalities, the emerging communist Poland ended up with a mainly homogeneous, ethnically Polish population (97.6% according to the December 1950 census).[[184\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_695-696-184)[[213\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-213) The remaining members of ethnic minorities were not encouraged, by the authorities or by their neighbors, to emphasize their ethnic identities.[[i\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_inone)[[a1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_a1none)

## Polish People's Republic (1945–1989)

### Post-war struggle for power

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Mikolajczyk.jpg/170px-Mikolajczyk.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mikolajczyk.jpg)

[Stanisław Mikołajczyk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanisław_Mikołajczyk)'s [Polish People's Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_People's_Party_(1945–1949)) [tried to outvote the communists in 1947](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1947_Polish_legislative_election), but the election process was rigged. Mikołajczyk had to flee to the West.

In response to the February 1945 [Yalta Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yalta_Conference) directives,[[180\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_6-7-180) a Polish [Provisional Government of National Unity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_National_Unity) was formed in June 1945 under Soviet auspices; it was soon recognized by the United States and many other countries.[[214\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-214) The Soviet domination was apparent from the beginning, as prominent leaders of the [Polish Underground State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Underground_State) were brought to trial in Moscow (the "[Trial of the Sixteen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trial_of_the_Sixteen)" of June 1945).[[215\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_8-215) In the immediate post-war years, the emerging communist rule was challenged by opposition groups, including militarily by the so-called "[cursed soldiers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cursed_soldiers)", of whom thousands perished in armed confrontations or were pursued by the [Ministry of Public Security](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ministry_of_Public_Security_(Poland)) and executed.[[216\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zamoyski_369-370-216) Such guerillas often pinned their hopes on expectations of an imminent outbreak of [World War III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_III) and defeat of the Soviet Union.[[217\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Dzień_Żołnierzy_Wyklętych-217) The [Polish right-wing insurgency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Military_Union) faded after the [amnesty of February 1947](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amnesty_of_1947).[[218\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zdobycie_władzy-218)[[219\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Po_wojnie_światowej_wojna_domowa-219)

The [Polish people's referendum of June 1946](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1946_Polish_people's_referendum) was arranged by the communist [Polish Workers' Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Workers'_Party) to legitimize its dominance in Polish politics and claim widespread support for the party's policies.[[220\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-220)[[221\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_9-221) Although the Yalta agreement called for free elections, the [Polish legislative election of January 1947](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1947_Polish_legislative_election) was controlled by the communists.[[216\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zamoyski_369-370-216) Some democratic and pro-Western elements, led by [Stanisław Mikołajczyk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanisław_Mikołajczyk), former prime minister-in-exile, participated in the Provisional Government and the 1947 elections, but were ultimately eliminated through [electoral fraud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electoral_fraud), intimidation and violence.[[216\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zamoyski_369-370-216) In times of severe political confrontation and radical economic change, members of Mikołajczyk's agrarian movement (the [Polish People's Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_People's_Party_(1945–1949))) attempted to preserve the existing aspects of [mixed economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mixed_economy) and protect property and other rights.[[222\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Buszko_417-425-222) However, after the 1947 elections, the Government of National Unity ceased to exist and the communists moved towards abolishing the post-war partially [pluralistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluralist_democracy) "people's democracy" and replacing it with a [state socialist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_socialism) system.[[223\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-223) The communist-dominated [front](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Popular_front) Democratic Bloc of the 1947 elections, turned into the [Front of National Unity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Front_of_National_Unity) in 1952, became officially the source of governmental authority. The [Polish government-in-exile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_government-in-exile), lacking international recognition, remained in continuous existence until 1990.

### Under Stalinism (1948–1955)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Boles%C5%82aw_Bierut_1949.jpg/170px-Boles%C5%82aw_Bierut_1949.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bolesław_Bierut_1949.jpg)

President [Bolesław Bierut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolesław_Bierut), leader of [Stalinist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stalinism) Poland

The [Polish People's Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_People's_Republic) (*Polska Rzeczpospolita Ludowa*) was established under the rule of the communist [Polish United Workers' Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_United_Workers'_Party) (PZPR). The name change from the Polish Republic was not officially adopted, however, until the proclamation of the [Constitution of the Polish People's Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Polish_People's_Republic) in 1952.[[224\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Sowa_178-179-224)

The ruling PZPR was formed by the forced amalgamation in December 1948 of the communist [Polish Workers' Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Workers'_Party) (PPR) and the historically non-communist [Polish Socialist Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Socialist_Party) (PPS). The PPR chief had been its wartime leader [Władysław Gomułka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Władysław_Gomułka), who in 1947 declared a "Polish road to socialism" as intended to curb, rather than eradicate, capitalist elements. In 1948 he was overruled, removed and imprisoned by [Stalinist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stalinism) authorities.[[225\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_26,_32-35-225)[[226\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_63-226) The PPS, re-established in 1944 by its left wing,[[227\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Ost_Solidarity_36-38-227) had since been allied with the communists.[[228\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Buszko_442-445-228)[[229\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_18,_39-229)[[230\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Lukowski_285-286-230) The ruling communists, who in post-war Poland preferred to use the term "socialism" instead of "communism" to identify their ideological basis,[[231\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_18-231)[[f\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_fnone) needed to include the socialist junior partner to broaden their appeal, claim greater legitimacy and eliminate competition on the political [Left](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Left-wing_politics). The socialists, who were losing their organization, were subjected to political pressure, ideological cleansing and purges in order to become suitable for unification on the terms of the PPR. The leading pro-communist leaders of the socialists were the prime ministers [Edward Osóbka-Morawski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Osóbka-Morawski) and [Józef Cyrankiewicz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Józef_Cyrankiewicz).[[228\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Buszko_442-445-228)[[230\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Lukowski_285-286-230)[[232\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Buszko_398-399,_407-232)

During the most oppressive phase of the Stalinist period (1948–1953), terror was justified in Poland as necessary to eliminate reactionary subversion. Many thousands of perceived opponents of the regime were arbitrarily tried and large numbers were executed.[[u\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_unone) The People's Republic was led by discredited Soviet operatives such as [Bolesław Bierut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolesław_Bierut), [Jakub Berman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jakub_Berman) and [Konstantin Rokossovsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konstantin_Rokossovsky).[[233\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_40-233) The independent [Catholic Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catholic_Church) in Poland was subjected to property confiscations and other curtailments from 1949, and in 1950 was pressured into signing an accord with the government.[[218\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zdobycie_władzy-218)[[219\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Po_wojnie_światowej_wojna_domowa-219) In 1953 and later, despite a partial thaw after the death of Stalin that year, the persecution of the Church intensified and its head, Cardinal [Stefan Wyszyński](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stefan_Wyszyński), was detained.[[234\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_66-68-234) A key event in the persecution of the Polish Church was the [Stalinist show trial of the Kraków Curia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stalinist_show_trial_of_the_Kraków_Curia) in January 1953.[[235\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-235)[[236\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Lukowski_286-292-236)[[237\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_39-48,_63-237)

In the [Warsaw Pact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Pact), formed in 1955, the Polish Army was the second largest, after the Soviet Army.[[238\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_434-238)

### Economic and social developments of the early communist era

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/42/Wyszy%C5%84ski.jpg/170px-Wyszy%C5%84ski.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Wyszyński.jpg)

[Primate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primate_(bishop)) [Stefan Wyszyński](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stefan_Wyszyński)'s leadership led to the exceptional strength of the Polish [Catholic Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catholic_Church)

In 1944, large agricultural holdings and former German property in Poland started to be redistributed through [land reform](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_reform), and industry started to be [nationalized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nationalization).[[222\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Buszko_417-425-222) Communist restructuring and the imposition of work-space rules encountered active worker opposition already in the years 1945–1947.[[239\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_24-26-239) The moderate [Three-Year Plan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Three-Year_Plan) of 1947–1949 continued with the rebuilding, [socialization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_ownership) and [socialist restructuring of the economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_economics). It was followed by the [Six-Year Plan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Six-Year_Plan) of 1950–1955 for [heavy industry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heavy_industry).[[218\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zdobycie_władzy-218) The rejection of the [Marshall Plan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marshall_Plan) in 1947 made aspirations for catching up with [West European](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Europe) standards of living unrealistic.[[240\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-240)[[241\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_12-16-241)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/A_Kult%C3%BAra_%C3%A9s_Tudom%C3%A1ny_Palot%C3%A1ja._Fortepan_75020.jpg/170px-A_Kult%C3%BAra_%C3%A9s_Tudom%C3%A1ny_Palot%C3%A1ja._Fortepan_75020.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:A_Kultúra_és_Tudomány_Palotája._Fortepan_75020.jpg)

Communist aspirations were symbolized by the [Palace of Culture and Science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palace_of_Culture_and_Science) in Warsaw

The government's highest economic priority was the development of heavy industry useful to the military. State-run or controlled institutions common in all the socialist countries of eastern Europe were imposed on Poland, including [collective farms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collective_farming) and [worker cooperatives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Worker_cooperative). The latter were dismantled in the late 1940s as not socialist enough, although they were later re-established; even small-scale private enterprises were eradicated.[[242\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_27,_39-242) Stalinism introduced heavy [political and ideological propaganda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Propaganda_in_the_Polish_People's_Republic) and [indoctrination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indoctrination) in social life, culture and education.[[236\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Lukowski_286-292-236)[[243\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_35-39-243)

Great strides were made, however, in the areas of employment (which became nearly full), [universal public education](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Education_in_the_Polish_People's_Republic) (which nearly eradicated adult illiteracy), health care and recreational amenities.[[244\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-244)[[245\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_22,_189-245) Many historic sites, including the central districts of Warsaw and Gdańsk, both devastated during the war, were rebuilt at great cost.[[246\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-246)[[247\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_21-22-247)

The communist industrialization program led to increased [urbanization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urbanization) and educational and career opportunities for the intended beneficiaries of the social transformation, along the lines of the peasants-workers-working intelligentsia paradigm. The most significant improvement was accomplished in the lives of Polish peasants, many of whom were able to leave their impoverished and overcrowded village communities for better conditions in urban centers. Those who stayed behind took advantage of the implementation of the 1944 [land reform decree of the Polish Committee of National Liberation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PKWN_Manifesto), which terminated the antiquated but widespread parafeudal socioeconomic relations in Poland. The Stalinist attempts at establishing collective farms generally failed. Due to urbanization, the national percentage of the rural population decreased in communist Poland by about 50%. A majority of Poland's residents of cities and towns still live in [apartment blocks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High-rise_building) built during the communist era, in part to accommodate migrants from rural areas.[[104\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Ziemia_dla_chłopów-104)[[248\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Główny_propagator_kapitalizmu-248)[[249\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Ostatni,_chłopi_nowoczesnej_Europy-249)

### The Thaw and Gomułka's Polish October (1955–1958)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Gomulka_speech.jpg/220px-Gomulka_speech.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gomulka_speech.jpg)

[Władysław Gomułka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Władysław_Gomułka) addressing the crowd in Warsaw in [October 1956](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_October)

In March 1956, after the [20th Congress of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/20th_Congress_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union) in Moscow ushered in [de-Stalinization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De-Stalinization), [Edward Ochab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Ochab) was chosen to replace the deceased Bolesław Bierut as first secretary of the Polish United Workers' Party.[[250\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_68-75-250) As a result, Poland was rapidly overtaken by social restlessness and reformist undertakings; thousands of political prisoners were released and many people previously persecuted were officially rehabilitated.[[251\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_76-86-251) [Worker riots in Poznań in June 1956](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poznań_1956_protests) were violently suppressed, but they gave rise to the formation of a reformist current within the communist party.[[252\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_86-92-252)[[253\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_24–25-253)

Amidst the continuing social and national upheaval, a further shakeup took place in the party leadership as part of what is known as the [Polish October](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_October) of 1956.[[254\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_96-104-254)[[k\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_knone) While retaining most traditional communist economic and social aims, the regime led by [Władysław Gomułka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Władysław_Gomułka), the new first secretary of the PZPR, liberalized internal life in Poland. The dependence on the Soviet Union was somewhat mollified, and the state's relationships with the Church and [Catholic lay activists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Znak_(association)) were put on a new footing.[[255\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_116-123-255) A repatriation agreement with the Soviet Union allowed the [repatriation of hundreds of thousands of Poles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Repatriation_of_Poles_(1955–59)) who were still in Soviet hands, including many former political prisoners.[[256\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_26-256) [Collectivization efforts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collectivization_in_the_Polish_People's_Republic) were abandoned—agricultural land, unlike in other [Comecon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comecon) countries, remained for the most part in the private ownership of farming families.[[256\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_26-256)[[257\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_80,_101-257) State-mandated provisions of agricultural products at fixed, artificially low prices were reduced, and from 1972 eliminated.[[258\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_36-258)

The [legislative election of 1957](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1957_Polish_legislative_election) was followed by several years of political stability that was accompanied by [economic stagnation and curtailment of reforms and reformists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_socialism). One of the last initiatives of the brief reform era was a nuclear weapons–free zone in Central Europe proposed in 1957 by [Adam Rapacki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adam_Rapacki), Poland's foreign minister.[[259\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_124-143-259)

[Culture in the Polish People's Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Culture_in_the_Polish_People's_Republic), to varying degrees linked to the [intelligentsia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intelligentsia)'s opposition to the authoritarian system, developed to a sophisticated level under Gomułka and his successors. The creative process was often compromised by [state censorship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Censorship_in_the_Polish_People's_Republic), but significant works were created in fields such as literature, theater, cinema and music, among others. Journalism of veiled understanding and varieties of native and Western [popular culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Popular_culture) were well represented. Uncensored information and works generated by [émigré](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Émigré) circles were conveyed through a variety of channels. The [Paris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paris)-based *[Kultura](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kultura)* magazine developed a conceptual framework for dealing with the issues of borders and the neighbors of a future free Poland, but for ordinary Poles [Radio Free Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radio_Free_Europe/Radio_Liberty) was of foremost importance.[[260\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-260)[[261\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_59-60-261)[[262\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Snyder_218-222-262)

### Stagnation and crackdown (1958–1970)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Bloki_ul._Pi%C4%85tkowska_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-Bloki_ul._Pi%C4%85tkowska_-_panoramio.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bloki_ul._Piątkowska_-_panoramio.jpg)

[Apartment blocks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High-rise_building) built in communist Poland (these located in [Poznań](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poznań))

One of the confirmations of the end of an era of greater tolerance was the expulsion from the communist party of several prominent "[Marxist revisionists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revisionism_(Marxism))" in the 1960s.[[259\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_124-143-259)

In 1965, the [Conference of Polish Bishops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Episcopal_Conference_of_Poland) issued the [Letter of Reconciliation of the Polish Bishops to the German Bishops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_of_Reconciliation_of_the_Polish_Bishops_to_the_German_Bishops), a gesture intended to heal bad mutual feelings left over from World War II.[[263\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_33-263) In 1966, the celebrations of the 1,000th anniversary of the [Christianization of Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christianization_of_Poland) led by Cardinal Stefan Wyszyński and other bishops turned into a huge demonstration of the power and popularity of the [Catholic Church in Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_Poland).[[264\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_I_15-16-264)

The post-1956 liberalizing trend, in decline for a number of years, was reversed in March 1968, when student demonstrations were suppressed during the [1968 Polish political crisis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1968_Polish_political_crisis).
