Motivated in part by the [Prague Spring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prague_Spring) movement, the Polish opposition leaders, intellectuals, academics and students used a historical-patriotic *[Dziady](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dziady_(poem))* theater spectacle series in Warsaw (and its termination forced by the authorities) as a springboard for protests, which soon spread to other centers of higher education and turned nationwide. The authorities responded with a major crackdown on opposition activity, including the firing of faculty and the dismissal of students at universities and other institutions of learning. At the center of the controversy was also the small number of Catholic deputies in the [Sejm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sejm) (the [Znak Association](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Znak_(association)) members) who attempted to defend the students.[[265\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_148-163-265)

In an official speech, Gomułka drew attention to the role of Jewish activists in the events taking place. This provided ammunition to a nationalistic and [antisemitic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antisemitism) communist party faction headed by [Mieczysław Moczar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mieczysław_Moczar) that was opposed to Gomułka's leadership. Using the context of the military victory of [Israel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel) in the [Six-Day War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Six-Day_War) of 1967, some in the Polish communist leadership waged an antisemitic campaign against the remnants of the Jewish community in Poland. The targets of this campaign were accused of disloyalty and active sympathy with Israeli aggression. Branded "[Zionists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zionism)", they were scapegoated and blamed for the unrest in March 1968, which eventually led to the emigration of much of Poland's remaining Jewish population (about 15,000 Polish citizens left the country).[[265\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_148-163-265)

With the active support of the Gomułka regime, the [Polish People's Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_People's_Army) took part in the infamous [Warsaw Pact invasion of Czechoslovakia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Pact_invasion_of_Czechoslovakia) in August 1968, after the [Brezhnev Doctrine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brezhnev_Doctrine) was informally announced.[[266\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_163-171-266)

In the final major achievement of Gomułka diplomacy, the governments of Poland and [West Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Germany) signed in December 1970 the [Treaty of Warsaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_Warsaw_(1970)), which normalized their relations and made possible meaningful cooperation in a number of areas of bilateral interest. In particular, West Germany recognized the post-World War II *de facto* border between Poland and [East Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Germany).[[267\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-267)[[268\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_177-180-268)

### Worker revolts, reforms of Gierek, the Polish pope and Solidarity (1970–1981)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Polish_1970_protests_-_Zbyszek_Godlewski_body.jpg/220px-Polish_1970_protests_-_Zbyszek_Godlewski_body.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Polish_1970_protests_-_Zbyszek_Godlewski_body.jpg)

One of the fatalities of the [1970 protests on the Baltic Coast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1970_Polish_protests)

Price increases for essential consumer goods triggered the [Polish protests of 1970](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1970_Polish_protests). In December, there were disturbances and strikes in the [Baltic Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baltic_Sea) port cities of [Gdańsk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gdańsk), [Gdynia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gdynia), and [Szczecin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Szczecin) that reflected deep dissatisfaction with living and working conditions in the country. The activity was centered in the industrial shipyard areas of the three coastal cities. Dozens of protesting workers and bystanders were killed in police and military actions, generally under the authority of Gomułka and Minister of Defense [Wojciech Jaruzelski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wojciech_Jaruzelski). In the aftermath, [Edward Gierek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Gierek) replaced Gomułka as first secretary of the communist party. The new regime was seen as more modern, friendly and pragmatic, and at first it enjoyed a degree of popular and foreign support.[[269\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_180-198-269)[[g\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_gnone)[[o\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_onone)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Gierek_in_Rz%C4%85%C5%9Bnik.jpg/220px-Gierek_in_Rz%C4%85%C5%9Bnik.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gierek_in_Rząśnik.jpg)

First Secretary [Edward Gierek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Gierek) (second from left) was unable to reverse Poland's economic decline

To revitalize the economy, from 1971 the Gierek regime introduced wide-ranging reforms that involved large-scale foreign borrowing. These actions initially caused improved conditions for consumers, but in a few years the strategy backfired and the economy deteriorated. Another attempt to raise food prices resulted in the [June 1976 protests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/June_1976_protests).[[270\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_198-206-270) The [Workers' Defence Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Workers'_Defence_Committee) (KOR), established in response to the crackdown that followed, consisted of dissident intellectuals determined to support industrial workers, farmers and students persecuted by the authorities.[[271\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-271)[[272\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_212-223-272) The opposition circles active in the late 1970s were emboldened by the [Helsinki Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helsinki_Accords) processes.[[270\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_198-206-270)

In October 1978, the [Archbishop of Kraków](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archbishop_of_Kraków), Cardinal Karol Józef Wojtyła, became [Pope John Paul II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II), head of the [Catholic Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catholic_Church). Catholics and others rejoiced at the elevation of a Pole to the [papacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pope) and greeted his June 1979 visit to Poland with an outpouring of emotion.[[273\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_228-229-273)

Fueled by large infusions of Western credit, Poland's economic growth rate was one of the world's highest during the first half of the 1970s, but much of the borrowed capital was misspent, and the centrally [planned economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planned_economy) was unable to use the new resources effectively. The [1973 oil crisis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1973_oil_crisis) caused [recession](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recession) and high interest rates in the West, to which the Polish government had to respond with sharp domestic consumer price increases. The growing debt burden became insupportable in the late 1970s, and negative economic growth set in by 1979.[[270\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_198-206-270)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Lech_Walesa_1980.jpg/170px-Lech_Walesa_1980.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lech_Walesa_1980.jpg)

[Lech Wałęsa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lech_Wałęsa) in 1980

Around 1 July 1980, with the Polish foreign debt standing at more than $20 billion, the government made yet another attempt to increase meat prices. Workers responded with escalating work stoppages that culminated in the [1980 general strikes in Lublin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lublin_1980_strikes).[[274\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_229-236-274) In mid-August, labor protests at the [Gdańsk Shipyard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gdańsk_Shipyard) gave rise to a chain reaction of strikes that virtually paralyzed the Baltic coast by the end of the month and, for the first time, closed most coal mines in [Silesia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silesia). The [Inter-Enterprise Strike Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inter-Enterprise_Strike_Committee) coordinated the strike action across hundreds of workplaces and formulated the [21 demands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/21_demands_of_MKS) as the basis for negotiations with the authorities. The Strike Committee was sovereign in its decision-making, but was aided by a team of "expert" advisers that included the well-known dissidents [Jacek Kuroń](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacek_Kuroń), [Karol Modzelewski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karol_Modzelewski), [Bronisław Geremek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronisław_Geremek) and [Tadeusz Mazowiecki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tadeusz_Mazowiecki).[[275\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_237-268-275)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Podpisanie_Porozumie%C5%84_Sierpniowych_w_Szczecinie.jpg/220px-Podpisanie_Porozumie%C5%84_Sierpniowych_w_Szczecinie.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Podpisanie_Porozumień_Sierpniowych_w_Szczecinie.jpg)

The signing of an [agreement between leaders of striking workers and government representatives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Szczecin_Agreement) in [Szczecin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Szczecin) in August 1980

On 31 August 1980, representatives of workers at the Gdańsk Shipyard, led by an electrician and activist [Lech Wałęsa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lech_Wałęsa), signed the [Gdańsk Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gdańsk_Agreement) with the government that ended their strike. Similar agreements were concluded in Szczecin (the [Szczecin Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Szczecin_Agreement)) and in Silesia. The key provision of these agreements was the guarantee of the workers' right to form independent [trade unions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trade_union) and the right to strike. Following the successful resolution of the largest labor confrontation in communist Poland's history, nationwide union organizing movements swept the country.[[275\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_237-268-275)

Edward Gierek was blamed by the Soviets for not following their "fraternal" advice, not shoring up the communist party and the official trade unions and allowing "anti-socialist" forces to emerge. On 5 September 1980, Gierek was replaced by [Stanisław Kania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanisław_Kania) as first secretary of the PZPR.[[276\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_269-272-276)

Delegates of the emergent worker committees from all over Poland gathered in Gdańsk on 17 September and decided to form a single national union organization named "[Solidarity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union))".[[277\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_44–45-277)

While party–controlled courts took up the contentious issues of Solidarity's legal registration as a trade union (finalized by November 10), planning had already begun for the imposition of [martial law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martial_law). A parallel farmers' union was organized and strongly opposed by the regime, but [Rural Solidarity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rural_Solidarity) was eventually registered (12 May 1981).[[278\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_52-278) In the meantime, a rapid deterioration of the authority of the communist party, disintegration of state power and escalation of demands and threats by the various Solidarity–affiliated groups were occurring.[[279\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_47-279) According to Kuroń, a "tremendous social democratization movement in all spheres" was taking place and could not be contained. Wałęsa had meetings with Kania, which brought no resolution to the impasse.[[280\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_272-301-280)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/00595309%28Andropov%26Jaruzelski%29.jpeg/220px-00595309%28Andropov%26Jaruzelski%29.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:00595309(Andropov%26Jaruzelski).jpeg)

General [Wojciech Jaruzelski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wojciech_Jaruzelski) meeting Soviet security chief [Yuri Andropov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuri_Andropov) during the 1980 crisis. Jaruzelski was about to become the (last) leader of communist Poland.

Following the Warsaw Pact summit in Moscow, the Soviet Union proceeded with a massive military build-up along Poland's border in December 1980, but during the summit Kania forcefully argued with [Leonid Brezhnev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonid_Brezhnev) and other allied communists leaders against the feasibility of an external military intervention, and no action was taken.[[280\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_272-301-280) The United States, under presidents [Jimmy Carter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jimmy_Carter) and [Ronald Reagan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ronald_Reagan), repeatedly warned the Soviets about the consequences of a direct intervention, while discouraging an open insurrection in Poland and signaling to the Polish opposition that there would be no rescue by the [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO) forces.[[281\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_302-307-281)

In February 1981, Defense Minister General [Wojciech Jaruzelski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wojciech_Jaruzelski) assumed the position of prime minister. The Solidarity social revolt had thus far been free of any major use of force, but in March 1981 in [Bydgoszcz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bydgoszcz) three activists were beaten up by the secret police. In a nationwide "warning strike" the 9.5-million-strong Solidarity union was supported by the population at large, but a general strike was called off by Wałęsa after the 30 March settlement with the government. Both Solidarity and the communist party were badly split and the Soviets were losing patience. Kania was re-elected at the Party Congress in July, but the collapse of the economy continued and so did the general disorder.[[282\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_307-325-282)

At the first [Solidarity National Congress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Solidarity) in September–October 1981 in Gdańsk, Lech Wałęsa was elected national chairman of the union with 55% of the vote. An appeal was issued to the workers of the other East European countries, urging them to follow in the footsteps of Solidarity.[[283\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_53-283) To the Soviets, the gathering was an "anti-socialist and anti-Soviet orgy" and the Polish communist leaders, increasingly led by Jaruzelski and General [Czesław Kiszczak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Czesław_Kiszczak), were ready to apply force.[[282\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_307-325-282)

In October 1981, Jaruzelski was named first secretary of the PZPR. The Plenum's vote was 180 to 4, and he kept his government posts. Jaruzelski asked parliament to ban strikes and allow him to exercise extraordinary powers, but when neither request was granted, he decided to proceed with his plans anyway.[[282\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_307-325-282)

### The martial law, Jaruzelski's rule and the end of communism (1981–1989)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/T-55A_Martial_law_Poland.jpg/220px-T-55A_Martial_law_Poland.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:T-55A_Martial_law_Poland.jpg)

[Martial law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martial_law_in_Poland) enforced in December 1981

On 12–13 December 1981, the regime declared [martial law in Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martial_law_in_Poland), under which the army and the [ZOMO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ZOMO) special police forces were used to crush Solidarity. The [Soviet leaders insisted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_reaction_to_the_Polish_crisis_of_1980–1981) that Jaruzelski pacifies the opposition with the forces at his disposal, without Soviet involvement. Almost all Solidarity leaders and many affiliated intellectuals were arrested or detained. Nine workers were killed in the [Pacification of Wujek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacification_of_Wujek). The United States and other Western countries responded by imposing economic sanctions against Poland and the Soviet Union. Unrest in the country was subdued, but continued.[[284\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_325-331-284)

During martial law, Poland was ruled by the so-called [Military Council of National Salvation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_Council_of_National_Salvation). The open or semi-open opposition communications, as recently practiced, were replaced by underground publishing (known in the eastern bloc as [Samizdat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samizdat)), and Solidarity was reduced to a few thousand underground activists.[[285\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_II_xxiii-285)[[286\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_332-360-286)

Having achieved some semblance of stability, the Polish regime relaxed and then rescinded martial law over several stages. By December 1982 martial law was suspended and a small number of political prisoners, including Wałęsa, were released. Although martial law formally ended in July 1983 and a partial amnesty was enacted, several hundred political prisoners remained in jail.[[284\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_325-331-284) [Jerzy Popiełuszko](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerzy_Popiełuszko), a popular pro-Solidarity priest, was abducted and murdered by security functionaries in October 1984.[[285\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_II_xxiii-285)[[286\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_332-360-286)

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Pope_John_Paul_II_11_06_1987_01.jpg/220px-Pope_John_Paul_II_11_06_1987_01.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pope_John_Paul_II_11_06_1987_01.jpg)

[Pope John Paul II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II) in Poland, 1987

Further developments in Poland occurred concurrently with and were influenced by the reformist leadership of [Mikhail Gorbachev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev) in the Soviet Union (processes known as [Glasnost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glasnost) and [Perestroika](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perestroika)).[[285\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_II_xxiii-285) In September 1986, a general amnesty was declared and the government released nearly all political prisoners. However, the country lacked basic stability, as the regime's efforts to organize society from the top down had failed, while the opposition's attempts at creating an "alternate society" were also unsuccessful.[[287\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_57-287) With the economic crisis unresolved and societal institutions dysfunctional, both the ruling establishment and the opposition began looking for ways out of the stalemate. Facilitated by the indispensable mediation of the Catholic Church, exploratory contacts were established.[[286\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_332-360-286)

Student protests resumed in February 1988. Continuing economic decline led to [strikes across the country](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1988_Polish_strikes) in April, May and August. The Soviet Union, increasingly destabilized, was unwilling to apply military or other pressure to prop up allied regimes in trouble.[[286\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_332-360-286)[[288\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_361-405-288) The Polish government felt compelled to negotiate with the opposition and in September 1988 preliminary talks with Solidarity leaders ensued in [Magdalenka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magdalenka,_Masovian_Voivodeship). Numerous meetings that took place involved Wałęsa and General Kiszczak, among others. In November, the regime made a major public relations mistake by allowing a televised debate between Wałęsa and [Alfred Miodowicz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_Miodowicz), chief of the [All-Poland Alliance of Trade Unions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/All-Poland_Alliance_of_Trade_Unions), the official trade union organization.[[289\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_58-99-289) The fitful bargaining and intra-party squabbling led to the official [Round Table Negotiations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Round_Table_Agreement) in 1989, followed by the [Polish legislative election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1989_Polish_legislative_election) in June of that year, a watershed event marking the [fall of communism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989) in Poland.[[288\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_361-405-288)[[289\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_58-99-289)

## Third Polish Republic (1989–today)

### Systemic transition

[![img](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Okr%C4%85g%C5%82y_St%C3%B3%C5%82_Pa%C5%82ac_Prezydencki_2019.jpg/220px-Okr%C4%85g%C5%82y_St%C3%B3%C5%82_Pa%C5%82ac_Prezydencki_2019.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Okrągły_Stół_Pałac_Prezydencki_2019.jpg)

The reconstructed [Polish Round Table](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Round_Table_Agreement) in the [Presidential Palace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidential_Palace,_Warsaw) where an agreement between the communists and the opposition was signed on 4 April 1989

The [Polish Round Table Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Round_Table_Agreement) of April 1989 called for local self-government, policies of [job guarantees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Job_guarantee), legalization of independent trade unions and many wide-ranging reforms.[[290\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_99–113-290) The current [Sejm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sejm) promptly implemented the deal and agreed to [National Assembly elections](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1989_Polish_legislative_election) that were set for 4 June and 18 June.[[291\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_115–123-291) Only 35% of the seats in the Sejm (national legislature's lower house) and all of the [Senate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Senate_of_Poland) seats were freely contested; the remaining Sejm seats (65%) were guaranteed for the communists and their allies.[[292\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_391-427-292)

The failure of the communists at the polls (almost all of the contested seats were won by the opposition) resulted in a political crisis. The new [April Novelization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/April_Novelization) to the constitution called for re-establishment of the Polish presidency and on 19 July the [National Assembly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contract_Sejm) [elected the communist leader](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1989_Polish_presidential_election), General [Wojciech Jaruzelski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wojciech_Jaruzelski), to that office. His election, seen at the time as politically necessary, was barely accomplished with tacit support from some Solidarity deputies, and the new president's position was not strong. Moreover, the unexpected definitiveness of the parliamentary election results created new political dynamics and attempts by the communists to form a government failed.[[291\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_115–123-291)[[292\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_391-427-292)[[293\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Historia_polityczna_42-51-293)

On 19 August, President Jaruzelski asked journalist and Solidarity activist [Tadeusz Mazowiecki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tadeusz_Mazowiecki) to form a government; on 12 September, the Sejm voted approval of Prime Minister Mazowiecki and his cabinet. Mazowiecki decided to leave the economic reform entirely in the hands of [economic liberals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_liberalism) led by the new Deputy Prime Minister [Leszek Balcerowicz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leszek_Balcerowicz),[[291\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_115–123-291) who proceeded with the design and implementation of his "[shock therapy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shock_therapy_(economics))" policy. For the first time in post-war history, Poland had a government led by non-communists, setting a precedent soon to be followed by other [Eastern Bloc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Bloc) nations in a phenomenon known as the [Revolutions of 1989](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989).[[292\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_391-427-292) Mazowiecki's acceptance of the "[thick line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thick_line)" formula meant that there would be no "[witch-hunt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witch-hunt#Figurative_use_of_the_term)", i.e., an absence of revenge seeking or exclusion from politics in regard to former communist officials.[[291\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_115–123-291)

In part because of the attempted [indexation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indexation) of wages, [inflation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inflation) reached 900% by the end of 1989, but was soon dealt with by means of radical methods. In December 1989, the Sejm approved the [Balcerowicz Plan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balcerowicz_Plan) to transform the Polish economy rapidly from a centrally planned one to a [free market](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_market) economy.[[294\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_125–130-294)[[v\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_vnone) The [Constitution of the Polish People's Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Polish_People's_Republic) was amended to eliminate references to the "leading role" of the communist party and the country was renamed the "Republic of Poland". The communist [Polish United Workers' Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_United_Workers'_Party) dissolved itself in January 1990. In its place, a new party, [Social Democracy of the Republic of Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_Democracy_of_the_Republic_of_Poland), was created.[[285\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_II_xxiii-285) "[Territorial self-government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_government)", abolished in 1950, was legislated back in March 1990, to be led by locally elected officials; its fundamental unit was the administratively independent [gmina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gmina).[[295\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_133–134-295)[[q\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#endnote_qnone)

In October 1990, the constitution was amended to curtail the term of President Jaruzelski. In November 1990, the [German–Polish Border Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German–Polish_Border_Treaty_(1990)), with [unified](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_reunification) [Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germany), was signed.[[296\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_138-296)

In November 1990, Lech Wałęsa [was elected president](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1990_Polish_presidential_election) for a five-year term; in December, he became the first popularly elected [president of Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_Poland). Poland's [first free parliamentary election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1991_Polish_parliamentary_election) was held in October 1991. 18 parties entered the new Sejm, but the largest representation received only 12% of the total vote.[[297\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_136–143-297)

### Democratic constitution, NATO and European Union memberships

There were several post-Solidarity governments between the [1989 election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1989_Polish_legislative_election) and the [1993 election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1993_Polish_parliamentary_election), after which the "[post-communist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratic_Left_Alliance)" left-wing parties took over.[[298\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_124-298) In 1993, the formerly Soviet [Northern Group of Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Group_of_Forces), a vestige of past domination, left Poland.[[285\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_II_xxiii-285)

In 1995, [Aleksander Kwaśniewski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aleksander_Kwaśniewski) of the [Social Democratic Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_Democracy_of_the_Republic_of_Poland) was elected president and remained in that capacity for the next ten years (two terms).[[285\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_II_xxiii-285)

In 1997, the new [Constitution of Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_Poland) was finalized and approved in a referendum; it replaced the [Small Constitution of 1992](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Small_Constitution_of_1992), an amended version of the [communist constitution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Polish_People's_Republic).[[299\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_152–156-299)

Poland joined [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO) in 1999.[[300\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_517-300) Elements of the [Polish Armed Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_Armed_Forces) have since participated in the [Iraq War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraq_War) and the [Afghanistan War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001–present)). Poland joined the [European Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Union) as part of its [enlargement in 2004](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2004_enlargement_of_the_European_Union).[[301\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-301) However, Poland [has not adopted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poland_and_the_euro) the [euro](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euro) as its currency and legal tender, but instead uses the [Polish złoty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_złoty).[[302\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-302)

In April 2010, Polish president [Lech Kaczynski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lech_Kaczyński) and dozens of the country's top political and military leaders died in the [Smolensk air disaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smolensk_air_disaster).[[303\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-303)

After the election of the conservative [Law and Justice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_and_Justice) party in 2015,[[304\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-304) the Polish government [repeatedly clashed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2015–present_Polish_constitutional_crisis) with EU institutions on the issue of judicial reform and was accused by the [European Commission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Commission) and the [European Parliament](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Parliament) of undermining "European Values" and eroding democratic standards. However, the Polish government headed by the Law and Justice party maintained that the reforms were necessary due to the prevalence of corruption within the Polish judiciary and the continued presence of holdover [Communist era](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_People's_Republic) judges.[[305\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-305)[[306\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-306)[[307\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-307)

In October 2019, Poland's governing Law and Justice party (PiS) won [parliamentary election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2019_Polish_parliamentary_election), keeping its majority in the lower house. The second was centrist [Civic Coalition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civic_Coalition_(Poland)) (KO). The government of Prime Minister [Mateusz Morawiecki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mateusz_Morawiecki) continued. However, PiS leader [Jaroslaw Kaczynski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jaroslaw_Kaczynski) was considered the most powerful political figure in Poland although not a member of government.[[308\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-308) In July 2020, President [Andrzej Duda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrzej_Duda), supported by PiS, was [re-elected](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2020_Polish_presidential_election).[[309\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-309)

## Notes

*a.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_anone) Piłsudski's family roots in the [Polonized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polonization) gentry of the [Grand Duchy of Lithuania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania) and the resulting perspective of seeing himself and people like him as legitimate Lithuanians put him in conflict with [modern Lithuanian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithuanian_National_Revival) nationalists (who in Piłsudski's lifetime redefined the scope and meaning of the "Lithuanian" identity), and, by extension, with other nationalists including the [Polish modern nationalist movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Democracy_(Poland)).[[310\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Snyder_40-41,_64-65,_68-69-310)

*b.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_bnone) In 1938, Poland and [Romania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romania) refused to agree to a [Franco](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/France)-[British](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom) proposal that in the event of war with [Nazi Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazi_Germany), [Soviet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union) forces would be allowed to cross their territories to aid [Czechoslovakia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Czechoslovakia). The Polish ruling elites considered the Soviets in some ways more threatening than the Nazis.[[311\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Heart_145-311)[[312\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_311-312)

The Soviet Union repeatedly declared intention to fulfill its obligations under the 1935 treaty with Czechoslovakia and defend Czechoslovakia militarily. A transfer of land and air forces through Poland and/or Romania was required and the Soviets approached about it the French, who also had a [treaty with Czechoslovakia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Locarno_Treaties) (and [with Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franco-Polish_alliance_(1921)) and [with the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franco-Soviet_Treaty_of_Mutual_Assistance)). [Edward Rydz-Śmigły](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Rydz-Śmigły) rebuked the French suggestion on that matter in 1936, and in 1938 [Józef Beck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Józef_Beck) pressured Romania not to allow even Soviet warplanes to fly over its territory. Like [Hungary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hungary), Poland was looking into using the [German-Czechoslovak conflict](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_occupation_of_Czechoslovakia) to settle its own territorial grievances, namely [disputes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish–Czechoslovak_border_conflicts) over parts of [Zaolzie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zaolzie), [Spiš](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiš) and [Orava](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orava_(region)).[[313\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-313)

*c.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_cnone) In October 1939, the [British Foreign Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Office) notified the Soviets that the United Kingdom would be satisfied with a postwar creation of small ethnic Poland, patterned after the [Duchy of Warsaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duchy_of_Warsaw).[[314\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_518-314) An establishment of Poland restricted to "minimal size", according to ethnographic boundaries (such as the lands common to both the prewar Poland and postwar Poland),[[315\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Overy_236_map-315) was planned by the Soviet [People's Commissariat for Foreign Affairs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_(Soviet_Union)) in 1943–1944. Such territorial reduction was recommended by [Ivan Maisky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ivan_Maisky) to [Vyacheslav Molotov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vyacheslav_Molotov) in early 1944, because of what Maisky saw as Poland's historically unfriendly disposition toward Russia and the Soviet Union, likely in some way to continue. [Joseph Stalin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Stalin) opted for a larger version, allowing a "swap" (territorial compensation for Poland), which involved the [eastern lands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kresy) gained by Poland at the [Peace of Riga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peace_of_Riga) of 1921 and now lost, and [eastern Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recovered_Territories) conquered from the Nazis in 1944–1945.[[316\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_1-3-316)[[317\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Kresowianie_nie_mieli_wyboru-317) In regard to the several major disputed areas: [Lower Silesia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lower_Silesia) west of the [Oder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oder) and the [Eastern Neisse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Neisse) rivers (the British wanted it to remain a part of the future German state),[[318\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-318) [Stettin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Szczecin) (in 1945 the German communists already established their administration there), "[Zakerzonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zakerzonia)" (western [Red Ruthenia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_Ruthenia) demanded by the Ukrainians), and the [Białystok](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Białystok) region (Białystok was claimed by the communists of the [Byelorussian SSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic)), the Soviet leader made decisions that favored Poland.

Other territorial and ethnic scenarios were also possible, generally with possible outcomes less advantageous to Poland than the form the country assumed.[[319\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Norman_Davies_1939-319)

*d.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_dnone) [Timothy D. Snyder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timothy_D._Snyder) spoke of about 100,000 Jews killed by Poles during the Nazi occupation, the majority probably by members of the [collaborationist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collaborationism) [Blue Police](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Police).[[320\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Polacy_wobec_Holocaustu-320) This number would have likely been many times higher had Poland entered into an alliance with Germany in 1939, as advocated by some Polish historians and others.

*e.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_enone) Some may have falsely claimed the [Jewish identity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_identity) hoping for permission to emigrate. The communist authorities, pursuing the concept of Poland of single ethnicity (in accordance with the recent border changes and expulsions),[[211\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Buszko_410-211)[[321\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Snyder_89-321) were allowing the Jews to leave the country.[[322\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_23-322) For a discussion of early communist Poland's ethnic politics, see [Timothy D. Snyder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timothy_D._Snyder), *The Reconstruction of Nations*, chapters on modern "Ukrainian Borderland".

*f.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_fnone) A [Communist Party of Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Poland) had [existed in the past](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communism_in_Poland), but was eliminated in [Stalin's purges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Purge) in 1938.[[323\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_18,_64-65-323)

*g.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_gnone) The Soviet leadership, which had previously ordered the crushing of the [Uprising of 1953 in East Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uprising_of_1953_in_East_Germany), the [Hungarian Revolution of 1956](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956) and the [Prague Spring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prague_Spring) in 1968, in late 1970 became worried about potential demoralizing effects that deployment against Polish workers would have on the Polish army, a crucial [Warsaw Pact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Pact) component. The Soviets withdrew their support for Gomułka, who insisted on the use of force; he and his close associates were subsequently ousted from the Polish [Politburo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politburo) by the Polish [Central Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Committee).[[324\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_57-59,_187,_196-324)

*h.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_hnone) East of the [Molotov-Ribbentrop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molotov–Ribbentrop_Pact) line, the population was 43% Polish, 33% Ukrainian, 8% Belarusian and 8% Jewish.[[325\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-325) The Soviet Union did not want to appear as an aggressor, and moved its troops to eastern Poland under the pretext of offering protection to "the kindred Ukrainian and Belorussian people".[[151\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Kochanski_59-93-151)

*i.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_inone) [Joseph Stalin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Stalin) at the 1943 [Tehran Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tehran_Conference) discussed with [Winston Churchill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winston_Churchill) and [Franklin D. Roosevelt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt) new post-war borders in central-eastern Europe, including the shape of a future Poland. He endorsed the [Piast Concept](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piast_Concept), which justified a massive shift of Poland's frontiers to the west.[[326\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-326) Stalin resolved to secure and stabilize the western reaches of the Soviet Union and disable the future military potential of Germany by constructing a compact and ethnically defined Poland (along with the Soviet ethnic Ukraine, Belarus and Lithuania) and by radically altering the region's system of national borders.[[327\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Snyder_179-187-327) After 1945, the Polish communist regime wholeheartedly adopted and promoted the Piast Concept, making it the centerpiece of their claim to be the true inheritors of Polish nationalism. After all the killings and population transfers during and after the war, the country was 99% "Polish".[[328\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-328)

*j.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_jnone) "All the currently available documents of Nazi administration show that, together with the Jews, the stratum of the Polish intelligentsia was marked for total extermination. In fact, Nazi Germany achieved this goal almost by half, since Poland lost 50 percent of her citizens with university diplomas and 35 percent of those with a [gimnazium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)) diploma."[[329\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-329) According to Brzoza and Sowa, 450,000 of Polish citizens had completed higher, secondary, or trade school education by the outbreak of the war. 37.5% of people with higher education perished, 30% of those with general secondary education, and 53.3% of trade school graduates.[[330\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_697-330)

*k.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_knone) Decisive political events took place in Poland shortly before the [Hungarian Revolution of 1956](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956). [Władysław Gomułka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Władysław_Gomułka), a reformist party leader, was reinstated to the [Politburo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politburo) of the [PZPR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PZPR) and the Eighth Plenum of its [Central Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Committee) was announced to convene on 19 October 1956, all without seeking a Soviet approval. The Soviet Union responded with military moves and intimidation and its "military-political delegation", led by [Nikita Khrushchev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev), quickly arrived in Warsaw. Gomułka tried to convince them of his loyalty but insisted on the reforms that he considered essential, including a replacement of Poland's Soviet-trusted minister of defense, [Konstantin Rokossovsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konstantin_Rokossovsky). The disconcerted Soviets returned to Moscow, the PZPR Plenum elected Gomułka first secretary and removed Rokossovsky from the Politburo. On 21 October, the Soviet Presidium [followed Khrushchev's lead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khrushchev_Thaw) and decided unanimously to "refrain from military intervention" in Poland, a decision likely influenced also by the ongoing preparations for the invasion of Hungary. The Soviet gamble paid off, because Gomułka in the coming years turned out to be a very dependable Soviet ally and an orthodox communist.[[254\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_96-104-254)

However, unlike the other [Warsaw Pact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Pact) countries, Poland did not endorse the Soviet armed intervention in Hungary. The Hungarian Revolution was intensely supported by the Polish public.[[331\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_114-116-331)

*l.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_lnone) The delayed reinforcements were coming and the government military commanders General [Tadeusz Rozwadowski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tadeusz_Jordan-Rozwadowski) and [Władysław Anders](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Władysław_Anders) wanted to keep on fighting the coup perpetrators, but President [Stanisław Wojciechowski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanisław_Wojciechowski) and the government decided to surrender to prevent the imminent spread of civil war. The coup brought to power the "[Sanation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanation)" regime under [Józef Piłsudski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Józef_Piłsudski) ([Edward Rydz-Śmigły](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Rydz-Śmigły) after Piłsudski's death). The Sanation regime persecuted the opposition within the military and in general. Rozwadowski died after abusive imprisonment, according to some accounts murdered.[[112\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Gwałt_i_ratunek-112) Another major opponent of Piłsudski, General [Włodzimierz Zagórski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Włodzimierz_Zagórski_(general)), disappeared in 1927.[[332\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Co_stalo_sie-332) According to [Aleksandra Piłsudska](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aleksandra_Piłsudska), the marshal's wife, following the coup and for the rest of his life Piłsudski lost his composure and appeared over-burdened.[[333\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-333)

At the time of Rydz-Śmigły's command, the Sanation camp embraced the ideology of [Roman Dmowski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Dmowski), Piłsudski's nemesis. Rydz-Śmigły did not allow General [Władysław Sikorski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Władysław_Sikorski), an enemy of the Sanation movement, to participate as a soldier in the country's defense against the [Invasion of Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland) in September 1939. During World War II in France and then in Britain, the [Polish government-in-exile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_government-in-exile) became dominated by anti-Sanation politicians. The perceived Sanation followers were in turn persecuted (in exile) under prime ministers Sikorski and [Stanisław Mikołajczyk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanisław_Mikołajczyk).[[334\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Rozliczanie_piłsudczyków-334)

*m.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_mnone) General [Zygmunt Berling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zygmunt_Berling) of the Soviet-allied [First Polish Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Polish_Army_(1944–1945)) attempted in mid-September a crossing of the [Vistula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vistula) and landing at [Czerniaków](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Czerniaków) to aid the insurgents, but the operation was defeated by the Germans and the Poles suffered heavy losses.[[335\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Kirchmayer_381-396-335)

*n.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_nnone) The decision to launch the [Warsaw Uprising](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Uprising) resulted in the destruction of the city, its population and its elites and has been a source of lasting controversy.[[336\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-The_Warsaw_Rising:_Was_it_all_worth_it?-336)[[337\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-The_Warsaw_Rising_1944:_Perception_and_Reality-337) According to the historians Czesław Brzoza and Andrzej Leon Sowa, orders of further military offensives, issued at the end of August 1944 as a continuation of [Operation Tempest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Tempest), show a loss of the sense of responsibility for the country's fate on the part of the underground Polish leadership.[[155\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_693–694-155)

*o.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_onone) One of the party leaders [Mieczysław Rakowski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mieczysław_Rakowski), who abandoned his mentor Gomułka following the 1970 crisis, saw the demands of the demonstrating workers as "exclusively socialist" in character, because of the way they were phrased.[[338\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_193-338) Most people in communist Poland, including opposition activists, did not question the supremacy of [socialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_socialism) or the [socialist idea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialism); misconduct by party officials, such as not following the provisions of the constitution, was blamed. From the time of Gierek, this assumed standard of [political correctness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_correctness) was increasingly challenged: [pluralism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluralism_(political_philosophy)), and then [free market](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_market), became frequently used concepts.[[339\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Poland_under_Communism_215-339)

*p.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_pnone) The Polish [Sanation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanation) authorities were provoked by the independence-seeking [Organization of Ukrainian Nationalists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organization_of_Ukrainian_Nationalists) (OUN). OUN engaged in political assassinations, terror and sabotage, to which the Polish state responded with a repressive campaign in the 1930s, as [Józef Piłsudski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Józef_Piłsudski) and his successors imposed [collective responsibility on the villagers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacification_of_Ukrainians_in_Eastern_Galicia) in the affected areas. After the disturbances of 1933 and 1934, the [Bereza Kartuska prison](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bereza_Kartuska_prison) camp was established; it became notorious for its brutal regime. The government brought Polish settlers and administrators to parts of [Volhynia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volhynia) with a centuries-old tradition of Ukrainian peasant rising against Polish land owners (and to Eastern [Galicia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galicia_(Eastern_Europe))). In the late 1930s, after Piłsudski's death, military persecution intensified and a policy of "national assimilation" was aggressively pursued. Military raids, public beatings, property confiscations and the closing and destruction of [Orthodox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church) churches aroused lasting enmity in Galicia and antagonized Ukrainian society in Volhynia at the worst possible moment, according to [Timothy D. Snyder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timothy_D._Snyder). However, he also notes that "Ukrainian terrorism and Polish reprisals touched only part of the population, leaving vast regions unaffected" and "the OUN's nationalist prescription, a Ukrainian state for ethnic Ukrainians alone was far from popular". [Halik Kochanski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halik_Kochanski) wrote of the legacy of bitterness between the Ukrainians and Poles that [soon exploded](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Massacres_of_Poles_in_Volhynia_and_Eastern_Galicia) in the context of [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II).[[340\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Snyder_143-152-340)[[341\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Kochanski_29-341) See also: [History of the Ukrainian minority in Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Ukrainian_minority_in_Poland#Second_Polish_Republic).

*q.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_qnone) In Poland, officials of central government (the provincial office of *[wojewoda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voivode#Poland)*) can overrule elected territorial and [municipal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Municipality) [local governments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_government). However, in such cases *wojewoda* decisions have sometimes been invalidated by courts.

*r.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_rnone) Foreign policy was one of the few governmental areas in which Piłsudski took an active interest. He saw Poland's role and opportunity as lying in Eastern Europe and advocated passive relations with the West. He felt that a German attack should not be feared, because even if this unlikely event were to take place, the Western powers would be bound to restrain Germany and come to Poland's rescue.[[342\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-342)

*s.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_snone) According to the researcher Jan Sowa, the Commonwealth failed as a state because it was not able to conform to the emerging new European order established at the [Peace of Westphalia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia) of 1648. Poland's elective kings, restricted by the self-serving and short-sighted nobility, could not impose a strong and efficient central government with its characteristic post-Westphalian internal and external sovereignty. The inability of Polish kings to levy and collect taxes (and therefore sustain a standing army) and conduct independent foreign policy were among the chief obstacles to Poland competing effectively on the changed European scene, where absolutist power was a prerequisite for survival and became the foundation for the abolition of [serfdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serfdom) and gradual formation of [parliamentarism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parliamentary_system).[[37\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Polska_urojona-37)

*t.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_tnone) Besides the [Home Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Home_Army) there were other major underground fighting formations: [Bataliony Chłopskie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bataliony_Chłopskie), [National Armed Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Armed_Forces) (NSZ) and [Gwardia Ludowa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gwardia_Ludowa) (later [Armia Ludowa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armia_Ludowa)).[[163\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-playground_344-346-163) From 1943, the leaders of the nationalistic NSZ collaborated with Nazi Germany in a case unique in occupied Poland. The NSZ conducted an anti-communist civil war. Before the arrival of the Soviets, the NSZ's [Holy Cross Mountains Brigade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holy_Cross_Mountains_Brigade) left Poland under the protection of the German army.[[343\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-343) According to the historians Czesław Brzoza and Andrzej Leon Sowa, participation figures given for the underground resistance are often inflated. In the spring of 1944, the time of the most extensive involvement of the underground organizations, there were most likely considerably fewer than 500,000 military and civilian personnel participating, over the entire spectrum, from the right wing to the communists.[[155\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Brzoza_Sowa_693–694-155)

*u.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_unone) According to [Jerzy Eisler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerzy_Eisler), about 1.1 million people may have been imprisoned or detained in 1944–1956 and about 50,000 may have died because of the struggle and persecution, including about 7,000 soldiers of the right-wing underground killed in the 1940s.[[218\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Zdobycie_władzy-218)[[219\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Po_wojnie_światowej_wojna_domowa-219) According to Adam Leszczyński, up to 30,000 people were killed by the communist regime during the first several years after the war.[[344\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Okupacja_ktorej_nie_bylo-344)

*v.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_vnone) According to [Andrzej Stelmachowski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrzej_Stelmachowski), one of the key participants of the Polish systemic transformation, Minister [Leszek Balcerowicz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leszek_Balcerowicz) pursued [extremely liberal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neoliberalism) [economic policies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balcerowicz_Plan), often extraordinarily painful for society. The December 1989 Sejm statute of credit relations reform introduced an "incredible" system of privileges for banks, which were allowed to unilaterally alter interest rates on already existing contracts. The exceedingly high rates they instantly introduced ruined many previously profitable enterprises and caused a complete breakdown of the apartment block construction industry, which had long-term deleterious effects on the state budget as well. Balcerowicz's policies also caused permanent damage to Polish agriculture, an area in which he lacked expertise, and to the often successful and useful Polish [cooperative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooperative) movement.[[294\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Stelmachowski_125–130-294)[[345\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-TINA_to_fałsz-345)

According to [Karol Modzelewski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karol_Modzelewski), a dissident and critic of the economic transformation, in 1989 [Solidarity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union)) no longer existed, having been in reality eliminated during the [martial law period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Poland_(1945–1989)#Final_decade_of_the_Polish_People's_Republic_(1980–90)). What the "post-Solidarity elites" did in 1989 amounted to a betrayal of the old Solidarity base, and the retribution was only a matter of time.[[346\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Celem_PiS_jest_władza-346)[[347\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Mamy_przechlapane-347)

*w.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_wnone) Led by [Władysław Anders](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Władysław_Anders), the [Polish II Corps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/II_Corps_(Poland)) fought in 1944–1945 in the Allied [Italian Campaign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_Campaign_(World_War_II)), where the corps' main engagement was the [Battle of Monte Cassino](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Monte_Cassino).[[348\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-348)

*x.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_xnone) The [Piast Concept](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piast_Concept), of which the chief proponent was [Jan Ludwik Popławski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jan_Ludwik_Popławski) (late 19th century), was based on the claim that the [Piast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piast_dynasty) homeland was inhabited by so-called "native" aboriginal Slavs and Slavonic Poles since time immemorial and only later was "infiltrated" by "alien" [Celts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celts), [Germanic peoples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germanic_peoples), and others. After 1945, the so-called "autochthonous" or "aboriginal" school of Polish prehistory received official backing and a considerable degree of popular support in Poland. According to this view, the [Lusatian Culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lusatian_Culture), which flourished between the [Oder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oder) and the [Vistula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vistula) in the early [Iron Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron_Age), was said to be Slavonic; all non-Slavonic tribes and peoples recorded in the area at various points in ancient times were dismissed as "migrants" and "visitors". In contrast, the critics of this theory, such as [Marija Gimbutas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marija_Gimbutas), regarded it as an unproved hypotheses and for them the date and origin of the westward migration of the Slavs were largely uncharted; the Slavonic connections of the Lusatian Culture were entirely imaginary; and the presence of an ethnically mixed and constantly changing collection of peoples on the [North European Plain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_European_Plain) was taken for granted.[[349\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Davies_Acta_Iaponica_Heritage-349)

*y.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_ynone) According to the count presented by Prime Minister and Internal Affairs Minister [Felicjan Sławoj Składkowski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felicjan_Sławoj_Składkowski) before the Sejm committee in January 1938, 818 people were killed in police suppression of labor protests (industrial and agricultural) during the 1932–1937 period.[[350\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Krwawe_strajki-350)

*z.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_znone) [John II Casimir Vasa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_II_Casimir_Vasa) is known for his remarkable and accurate prediction of the [Partitions of Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland), made over a century before the event's occurrence.[[37\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Polska_urojona-37)

*a1.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_a1none) According to war historian [Ben Macintyre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ben_Macintyre), "The Polish contribution to allied victory in the Second World War was extraordinary, perhaps even decisive, but for many years it was disgracefully played down, obscured by the politics of the [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War)."[[351\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-351)

*b1.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_b1none) Piłsudski left the Polish Socialist Party in 1914 and severed his connections with the socialist movement, but many activists from the Left and of other political orientations presumed his continuing involvement there.[[352\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-352)

*c1.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_c1none) [Woodrow Wilson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson)'s [Fourteen Points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fourteen_Points) program was subsequently weakened by internal developments in the US, Britain, France, and Germany. In the last case, Poland was denied the city of [Danzig](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gdańsk) on the [Baltic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baltic_Sea) coast.[[80\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-Versailles-80)

*d1.*[**^**](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#ref_d1none) The government of [Soviet Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Soviet_Russia_and_the_Soviet_Union_(1917–27)) issued in August 1918 a decree strongly supportive of the independence of Poland, but at that time no Polish lands were under Russian control.[[353\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Poland&printable=yes#cite_note-353)
